Taiwan is referenced because various Chinese immigrants settled there and adapted their cooking to the local tastes and ingredients. They created â€œMongolian barbecueâ€ (grilling meats and vegetables on large, flat iron griddles). This is an inventive take on stir-frying techniques common in Chinese cuisine. It was then taken to the US.

My grandmother was one of the first Chinese chefs to appear on American TV, in Seattle back in the 1960s.  Decades later, she attended a presentation by Martin Yan in Hawaii. He did his dish then said he was going to choose someone from the audience to prepare something. He chose my grandmother. Later, she asked him why. He said â€œyou were the only one not taking notes. I figure you must know something.â€  In fact, my grandmother grew up in China in a wealthy family and was taught by master chefs in China. She actually didnâ€™t want to learn, thinking she would always have cooks, but her father was involved in politics and could see the growing connection between China and the West. Her mother thought she might wind up living in the West and would need to know how to cook. My grandmother wound up spending about 50 years I America, cooking fantastic Chinese food for all of them.

.Baking sodaÂ acts as a meat tenderizer by changing the physical composition of meat fibers. It raises the pH levels on the surface and makes it tougher for the proteins in the meat to bond. Corn starch makes a barrier and traps in moisture to make it tender and helps with overcooking the beef.

Saturday mornings, after cartoons were done, PBS time and it was Martin Yan, Justin Wilson, and Julia  Child exposing my tiny rural Indiana brain  to culture and flavor. His skill with a knife and his positive, affirming voice were mesmerizing. That szying "If Yan can cook, you can too!" Was burned into my brain and inspired me to try tasting and cooking food from cultures I had no experience with. It led to a lifelong love of cooking and an openness to new things that would lead me out of my little rural town and into a much wider world.  Legend almost seems to humble.

Martin Yan is a true treasure.  He lived nearby and his shows were all taped at KQED in San Francisco. Interesting & fun facts; the real OG TV chef named Yan was Stephen Yan. Funnily enough, aside from the same name & skill set, they also have the same sense of humour, very punny.  Stephen's first TV series was made in Vancouver, BC, & was called Wok with Yan. Every episode he wore an apron with a new pun using the word wok. Martin also got his start on TV in Canada, guesting on a talkshow in Calgary, AB. Martin actually worked for Stephen & i can't help but wonder if he followed Stephen's lead on fitting punny humour into his shows. They are both great, but i have a special place in my heart for Stephen. I remember the anticipation at the start of every episode, waiting for him to reveal the day's joke apron.  It's silly, but, as a Canadian, am kinda proud Canada can lay claim to both of these funny, talented chefs. BTW, your ".... you're Peking my interest." comment was worthy of both Yan punsters. ğŸ˜†

My dad met Martin Yan when Martin was flying out of SFO to Hong Kong on one of his many trips.  Martin gave my dad an autographed copy of one of his cook books and my dad used the hellout of that book.  Our favorite dish was mango beef, my dad made it so many times he could do it from memory.  Even years after my dad had passed, we keep that cookbook in the same place my dad kept it because of how much it meant to our dad and because it it has been an oddly important part of our kitchen for the last 30 years.

I LOVED Yan Can Cook!  And his show was absolutely groundbreaking-- basically the Julia Child of Chinese cooking, because he showed the average (North) American home cook that Chinese cuisine could be both approachable and healthy.  (Not that it wasn't, but he was working against some pretty entrenched stereotypes.)  My mother bought her first wok because of him.

Does anyone remember Stephen Yan? He hosted an 80's cooking show on CBS titled, Wok with Yan. He had a different pun written on his apron for each episode, such as Wok Don't Run, and Wokkey Night in Canada. When the cook was complete, he would invite an audience member to eat with him, and always opened a fortune cookie before the meal. His show and Yan Can Cook are similar. and though not related, Martin Yan, had once worked for Stephen Yan, as a demonstrator for Stephen's products.

I grew up largely in the 90's on public network television. Children's programming was often pretty bland and boring at the time, so Yan Can Cook and the Frugal Gourmet were frequent staples of mine and my brother's daytime entertainment. The good stuff like Star Trek and the more interesting educational programming wasn't on until later, and over time that time slot was consumed by my siblings and my parents with boring, vapid nonsense like sports and talent show programs. So naturally, as I grew and was forced to seek entertainment from my hobbies and my computer rather than the TV, cooking and international food programs were always one of my primary fixtures.   Martin Yan was always at the epicenter of that.
